Date: Wed, 17 Mar 93 17:13:00 -0600
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: 914 power

>Thanks for reminding me to ask this, Stan.  I'm a little confused about
>dry-sumps.  First, are they legal in SCCA Solo II Street Prepared?  If so,
>what's the real difference between a dry-sump setup and an Accusump?

Don't know what the legalities are in Solo II, but Accusumps are allowed in all
classes of road racing, and dry sumps are allowed to be retrofitted in SCCA
Production and above. 

Now, what's the difference?  Accusump and dry sump solve some of the same 
problems in a very different way. Both provide a method for keeping pressure
to the bearings under almost all conditions, both provide an extra  N
quarts of oil (2 <= N <= 10), and both provide an extra "heat sink" capability
to cool the extra oil.

An Accusump is a pressurized cylinder (using air pressure) that stores oil
when you have more pressure from the oil pump than air pressure, and sources
oil under pressure when you don't. Easiest way to install an Accusump on 
914/924/944 variants is to get an spin-on oil filter remote plate (gizmo with
oil filter threads on one side and 2 5/8" NPT ports on the other), the
right fittings for that, a similar sized "T" fitting, some hose, a remote
oil filter mount kit, a one-way check valve, and an Accusump. Plumb this with
the pressure port of the oil filter replacement plate going to the oil filter
remote thing, then the check valve, then the "T", then back to the engine. On 
the "other side" of the T, put the Accusump. BTW, you can also add an oil
cooler at this time -- in the return loop, on the other side of the Accusump. 
Anndd, this is a *GREAT* time to toss you old spin-on filter and use a *REAL* 
filter, the Canton/MECA replaceable cartridge unit. Oh, doing this on a 
356 involves machine work and welding to add a "full flow filter" instead of 
the normal bypass filter, and you don't do it at all for 911s. And, if you do 
an get an Accusump, get the 3 quart version.

Dry sump uses at least two oil pumps, and cannot be installed without
case dissassembly and modification. The first pump, the scavenge pump,
picks up oil from the sump and shoots it to a storage tank. The second
pump, the pressure pump, picks up oil from the storage tank and send it 
into the engine. The scavenge pump must be about 2x the capacity of the
pressure pump, which doesn't need to be as big as it does in the "normal"
wet sump case as it's getting oil under gravity feed from a tank. There
are some wierd things you need to do to make dry sump work really well, 
like electonicly controlled cutoff valves in the line from the tank to
the pressure pump (so you don't drain the tank into the sump when you
cut the engine off), a de-foaming tank prior to the storage tank, and the 
usual oil cooler/oil filter stuff. Like I said, you have to modify the
case to change the way that oil flows from the pump, and you usually
have to do welding and machining for the extra fittings you need to use
to move the oil around. 914's are relatively easy to dry sump, as it's
pretty similar to doing same to a Type 1 VW engine and there are lots of
parts available in the SCORE/HDRA world for this. Doing 924/944s is harder,
but you get to use external belt-driven pumps, which you can get in the
Formula car or NASCAR world. Doing 356s is just about impossible. And 911s
come that way from the factory.

>In short, I have the following three options.
> (1) leave it alone
> (2) Accusump
> (3) dry sump
>
>Which should I go with?  What are the pros and cons of each, and what is the
>expense and work involved?  This is for a dedicated autocrosser.

The above descriptions should give you an idea what's going on. You can
install an Accusump in a 914 in a morning, and a total cost of under $300
(using barbed fittings, hose clamps, and regular rubber oil pressure hose,
and assuming you get the 3 qt. with the electronic valve. Add another $90
for the Canton/MECA oil filter, and what, $150 for an oil cooler) 

Installing a dry sump in a 914 usually costs about $1100 in parts, welding,
and machining, not including the engine re-build you sort of get the 
opportunity to do while you have the case apart. And takes, well, a long time.

My street 914 has the standard wet sump.  My ITA 914 has an Accusump. The 
Ghost Racing EP 914 is dry sump.  Your choice.

Stan

Date: Wed, 17 Mar 93 23:52:28 -0600
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: more 914 power

>...however it seems that it
>will be a whole lot of money for limited power (still stuck under 200 hp).

Before you decide that the "limited power" of a reasonable 2.0 914 is not
enough for you, let me encourage you to see about getting a ride in someone's
car that makes power in that range. You'll be really surprised. About 150 HP
in a 914 feels more like about 400 in a medium-to-large American-type car
(Yup. I used to build them too.) Not to mention the *HUGE* performance 
advantage you already have with a *LIGHT* mid-engine car, with a *VERY LIGHT*
engine. 

>So it seems to me to be time to ditch the 2.0 motor and get something more 
>substantial.  I've heard of a few places who have engine conversion kits for
>V-6, V-8, and rotary motors.  I like the rotary idea for the light weight and 
>small size, but have both V-6's and V-8's laying around waiting to be put in 
>something.  I sent for info from Hybrid Cars and Kennedy Engineering today, 
>what other companies have these kits?  Does anybody have experience with these
>companies or conversions?  

Well, there's one guy in the Houston area who has a Chevy 350 in his 914, but
everytime he comes to an autocross, he blows something -- oil lines, water
lines, head gaskets, etc. 

Second caveat on that: when you change the engine, you *WILL* screw up the 
suspension and brakes. You basicly have to re-engineer the whole car to deal
with a new weight and more imporantly, weight distribution. Not to mention
the various reinforcing you have to do to the tub to keep from getting that
600 pound V8 in the front seat with you... And all that will get expensive in
a hurry -- worst case is you'll need to semi-tube frame the car, which should
only be about $6k, plus needing *REAL* brakes (vented 20 or 24 mm rotors with
large calipers). If you use Porsche A or S calipers, you can do that for about
$4k (you'll need the new front end parts, and Vellios's trick $1k rear trailing
arms that take 911S or SC rear brakes); if you use Bremtek, it's about the
same but you get bigger brakes; if you use JFZ or Wilwood, you can do it for
less but will have to engineer some parts needed to make it work.

>How is the suspention affected?  

Simple. More weight, in a different location, will change the spring rates 
and dampning needed to keep the car stable under all directional loading.
Not to mention changing the rates needed to give you a given ride height.

>Are there any other ways of getting decent horsepower?

Look, you're about to spend a zillion dollars on putting something together
which will only be unstable and *EXTREMELY* expensive to maintain. So why
not take a different approach? 

Sell your 914. You should be able to get $3-5k for it, depending. 

Then go buy (your choice): 

* an MR-2 Turbo, about $8-10K; 
* a 911SC, about $9-15K;
* an early ('71 or '73) 911S (*BIG* horsepower, light car), about $10K. 

Or better yet, get a penis extention, since that seems to be what you're
really looking for... 8{) (/2).

Stan

Rod Simpson (or Stimson?) is the originator of the Porsche/V8
conversion. His company is called Renegade Hybrids and from what
I have heard he is the best source for kits/parts/service of
these animals. Sorry I don't know his number but he is located in
Southern CA, LA area. Apparently he has been doing this since the
sixties and he's the ultimate guru.

My opinion on this has flip-flopped over the years. I am now at
the stage where I think it's a bad idea, for many of the same
reasons Stan has mentioned, namely cost and general ugliness
of the finished product as far as integration of all the elements
is concerned. I admit to only having seen one of these beasts
in person and that one was a complete butcher job. Basically to
do it right you are going spend *big* bucks.

A far better option is to *bolt* in a Porsche 6 cylinder. 
Expensive, yes, but not so much when you consider the completed
job will be far more smooth than a V8 shoehorn job. Also,
the 901 trans is capable of staying together with a hi-po
2.7 ltr whereas the stock trans would blow up with a V8.
Consider also the handling aspect, the flat-6 is a feather-
weight compared to a fat Rat...

Date: Thu, 18 Mar 93 13:31:06 EST
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: Lowering a '73 911T


   could you elaborate on the procedure?  i'd like to lower the front end of
   my 912 about 1/4" - 1/2".

Sure. Keep in mind this procedure does not include alignment and
balancing for optimal handling:

Instructions for adjusting front ride height 914/911(65-89):

The front end of the 914/911 suspension consists of two lateral
torsion bars that are splined at each end. The front end (toward the
front of the car) is fixed and the rear end is set into an adjuster
which maintains a variable pre-load which is used to adjust the
ride height. Adjustment is made by simply changing the arm length
of the adjuster. It is really very simple when you look at it.

1. Locate the adjuster. It is at the rear of the torsion bar, one
for each side. The point you are going to turn is a threaded
shaft with an 11mm head for a wrench or socket. The head is facing
down. Immediately aft of this piece is the rear bushing for the
A-arm.

2. Turn the adjuster. Tightening (turn clockwise) will raise the
front. 

3. Loosening (anti-clockwise) will lower the car. 

All of the above should be done with the front wheels on the ground
if possible and in small increments. Count the number of 1/4 turns
you make on each side so you can bring it back if you need to. After
each turn,  shake the car up and down to settle the suspension. It
may take a few miles of driving to actually realize the new ride 
height, after which you might need to adjust again.
 
If you get to the end of the adjustment range and find that the car
is not at the height you require, then you have to reset the torsion
bar on its splines. The following description applies to this case
only and is *much* easier to follow if you are looking at the parts:

Instructions to reset the front torsion bars.

1. Jack up the front of the car. Place on stand. 

2. Completely remove the adjuster bolt and the adjuster from the
rear of the torsion bar. 

3. Mark the position of the torsion bar against the crossmember.

4. Pull the torsion bar readward until it is unseated from the 
front splines. Now, looking at the torsion bar along its length
from the rear, turn one spline clockwise to lower or one spline
anti-clockwise to raise. One spline should be enough for even the
most severe alterations.

5. Replace the adjuster on the rear splines in a position so that
you can put the adjuster bolt back in.

NOTE: The number of splines in the front of the bar is different
from the number of splines at the rear of the bar. This gives a 
fine degree of adjustment when resetting the bar.

Enjoy!

- --
- ----------------------------------------------------------
John Colasante                   johnc@gdstech.grumman.com

Date: Thu, 18 Mar 93 13:48:00 EST
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: 914 power


   What do you need besides the motor? Headers, exhaust, oil tank?

Front engine mount. The original engine mount on the 914-6 is a
single centered mount that is welded to the firewall. Conversions
typically use an "engine bar" which mounts to either side if the
914 chassis, just like on the 914-4. This is a superior mount in
many ways. If you go this route you'll need a modified shift
linkage bar. 

914-6 Engine sheet metal.

Various and sundry oil lines.

Depending on the engine choice a new flywheel and clutch.

Date: Thu, 18 Mar 93 16:10:15 -0600
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: 914 power

>First of all - I am not trying to be some arbiter of taste but
>Stan's remark re: anatomical addititions was out of place,
>IMHO.

Yeah, you're right. I blew it. Oh well. Now on to other matters.

>Now that is off my chest:
>The 914-6 is a sweet, extraodinarily well balanced 'go
>so fast your heart cramps' car. 
>What do you end up missing
>if you put a 911 motor in a 914-4. Are the 914-6's running
>gear, etc very different? I seem to recall they are much the 
>same.
>
>What do you need besides the motor? Headers, exhaust, oil tank?
>I would think, with the scarcity of 914-6 and the plentitude
>of 914-4 this would be a natural. Used 911 motors aren't all
>that expensive if you look around.

You need:

* headers
* electric heater setup to compensate for the fact that you can't 
  get 914/6 heat exchangers (if you want heat)
* oil tank (better plan on using aftermarket in non-stock location)
* oil lines and misc. plumbing -- filter mount, etc. (made to mate with 
  your tank, etc.)
* fuel system -- lots of choices here, Weber 40IDA3C being "canonical
  choice" but CIS and "custom EFI" being OK as well
* mods to allow you to have a hand throttle and choke actuator
* wiring harness
* engine sheet metal
* motor mount bar ("Quick-6" or similar)
* maybe new flywheel, clutch, etc. depending on choice of engine
* maybe new starter depending on choice of flywheel
* maybe suspension upgrade, depending
* maybe brakes upgrade, depending
* maybe alternator upgrade, depending

You can get most of this stuff as a kit (well, semi-kit) either from
George Stevens at Stevens Machining/RadTechRacing, or Mid-Engine Mania.
The hard part is dealing with the oil system. You just can't get stock
tanks anymore. So you need to use a non-stock dry sump tank, and that
means mounting in a non-stock location. What do choose depends on any
number of things. I personally like the Stevens tank, which replaces the
whole stock battery mount position (sheet metal and all) with a 20 quart
tank, using AN fittings. He also has 911-to-AN conversion gizmos you need
to make the lines up, but it means you can use fabricated Aeroquip lines
instead of having to figure out how to adapt the Porsche parts.

Or, you can wander off into racing land, and snarf some of the solutions
the guys building GT-2 914/6's use. In any event, you give up the front
trunk as you either put the battery there or the oil tank there.

I may have forgotten a few parts, and I have *CERTAINLY* glossed the
details of how to do a conversion. 

If you get serious about doing a conversion, give me a shout and I can
dig out my notebooks, and go from there.

Stan

Subject: 944 sounds

Hello

My 87 944S has always made a nice low rattle when in neutral, clutch  
in. I have always thought that this was just the gears rattling, and  
was not a problem.

Recently however, the car has been doing slightly funny things. Like  
when I am under full acceleration, the car vibrates a bit. Also, I  
can feel the syncros when I shift (not much, but noticible after  
driving my VW). The final thing that worried me was a low whine that  
appeared to be coming from under the car when I pulled into the  
driveway (in neutral, clutch in). A few clutch pumps, and it was  
gone, but...

Could my clutch release bearing be on the way out, or am I just being  
paraniod? Also, what is the reccommended interval for replacing the  
clutch hydraulic fluid, and would old fluid contribute to (slightly)  
rough shifting. If the bearing, how difficult is it for the home  
mechanic to replace (standard tools, no jack, cold outside)? Is it  
something that I can ignore till spring?

On the other hand, this might be just be a case of my driving a BMW  
for a week, and I am overreacting. Then again, it might be something  
serious, like my clutch is worn out and needs replacement, or the  
bearing, or motor mounts, etc... That doesn't seem too likely since I  
haven't felt it slip, but the vibration on hard accell makes me  
wonder...

CZ

Date: Fri Mar 19 09:25:04 1993
From: xgg2356@dcmdc.dla.mil (James Fuerstenberg)
Subject: Re: Porschephiles #336 Thu Mar 18 23:00:01 CST 1993

Re: 914 power.

Yes, the V-8 conversion can be done and done well.  A friend of mine has
a very well done 283 chevy ( built!) in a 914.  The car looks stock but is
VERY fast.  However, the cost to do a similar conversion CORRECTLY is at
least as much as necessary to buy a real 914-6 outright.

I owned a 914-6 which I had replaced the 2liter 6 with a 2.7 and aluminum 
flywheel and webers instead of fuel injection.  The car looked stock, but
was very fast...considerably quicker than my 911SC.  I was able to outdrag
late model corvettes out of the tollbooths (not that I would ever do or
condone a little street racing). One can buy 914-6s with bigger motors for
far less than it takes to do the conversion oneself.  Check the Panorama and
Excellence Ads regularly and be patient.

I have seen 3liter 914-6 conversions which were blindingly fast and not all
that expensive when it came time for sale.

jim

Date: Fri, 19 Mar 93 12:18:40 EST
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: 944 sounds


   > My 87 944S has always made a nice low rattle when in neutral, clutch  
   > in. I have always thought that this was just the gears rattling, and  
   > was not a problem.

   Yeah, mine does that too - the garage says they all do.

I'm not sure if this applies to the '87 944S but:

One failure mode of the 944 clutch is that the rubber center of the
clutch disk disintegrates partially long before the linings are
worn down. I don't know why this happens but it manifests itself
as a vibration that occurs at various engine speeds. If the rubber
is completely gone then the clutch will begin to make rude clunking
noises.

One thing to try is opening the plastic access port under the car 
at the transmission end and grab the driveshaft. There should be
zero play in the driveshaft. If there is any play the rubber center
might be shot. 


- --
- ----------------------------------------------------------
John Colasante                   johnc@gdstech.grumman.com

stock US '77 930 to the Euro version.  I anticipate that this will improve
performance below 3K RPM. Bruce Anderson's book also says that
this is one of the first things to do on the way to better performance.
However, the book doesn't elaborate on how much of a job this is. Is it a
matter of unbolting the stock US headers from the heads, turbocharger, 
and wastegate and bolting-on the Euro version? I'm not that lucky!

I can see 4 areas where problems could potentially occur. This is at
the turbocharger, the wastegate, the muffler, and the oil return
line. From photos in Mr. Anderson's book, it appears that the turbocharger
attachment point is the same and that the wastegate bolts-on in a new
place and has its own small muffler. This means that the muffler
must change or that the wastegate outlet pipe attached to the US
muffler must be plugged (if this is even possible). Finally, the
return oil line that goes from the left front side of the engine across
to the oil tank appears to be in the way of the Euro exhaust's left
side crossover pipe. Does this oil line have to be replaced too?

I've also noticed a lot of stainless steel headers on the market and
wondered what these had to offer over the Porsche Euro exhaust. In addition
to looks and longevity, is there more?

Anyway, thanks for your help.

Date: Sat, 20 Mar 1993 06:42:41 -0500
From: tomh@metrics.com (Tom Haapanen)
Subject: Re: 914/6' and various motors

Chuck Canale wrote:
> Are 914/6 2.2's taken straight from early 911 motors?

It's a 2.0L, but, yes, it's basically the early 911 motor.

> Does anyone have any info on the 914/8's made for Ferry and Ferdinand by
> the factory circa 1973?

Well, I've seen Ferry Porsche's car (the other was built for Ferdinad Piech,
then the chief of development at Porsche, and now the chairman of Volkswagen
AG) at the Porsche Museum at the factory.  It's a nice-looking car, with
little touches like wide pop-up headlights.  The engine is a Porsche 908
motor; Piech's was pretty much in racing tune, while Ferry Porsche's was
detuned for better driveability.

> Thanks for the answers, Chuck "Wanting a bigger penis" Canale
 
I'm happy with my 2.0L one...  :)

------------------------------

Date: Sat, 20 Mar 93 13:13:01 EST
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)


   muffler must be plugged (if this is even possible). Finally, the
   return oil line that goes from the left front side of the engine across
   to the oil tank appears to be in the way of the Euro exhaust's left
   side crossover pipe. Does this oil line have to be replaced too?

You will need replace two lines. The main line from the engine that
you mentioned can be replaced with one from the '74 911 which wraps
around the bottom of the transmission instead of going past the exhaust.
The part is PN 911.107.739.10. This is a solid line. Then you need the
line from here to the thermostat which has a rubber part to it,
PN 911.107.729.10. Please make sure you check this out before ordering
because I might be off on the exact PN but it's a good starting point.
This is the same conversion you would need if you mounted early
exhaust on an SC or Carrera with a front cooler.
- --
- ----------------------------------------------------------
John Colasante                   johnc@gdstech.grumman.com

Date: Sat, 20 Mar 93 13:01:30 EST
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: 914 power


   That's one thing I was expecting, which is why I prefer the rotary idea.  I'm 
   not that familiar with them, but they seem to have the size and weight I want.
   Is anyone familiar enough with rotarys to tell me if it would be a good choice?
   I recieved no responses on that, but I'd appreciate some.  Anyway, with the 
   mid-engine design and foward radiator placement, the weight distribution would
   not be altered that much, even with the V-8.

The Mazda rotary would be an excellent choice for such a conversion,
and if you did it you wouldn't be the first. Kennedy Engineering in
CA makes an adaptor plate to mount the Mazda rotary to the 901 trans.
Also, Beetle owners have done this for years.
The Mazda rotary comes in two forms, the 12A (early) and the 13B. The
13B has been used in RX-7's since 1986. I'm not considering the very
first rotaries from the early 70's here. You want to look at the 13B
because it's more robust and has a larger "displacement". Also, there
is a turbo variant that is quite powerful.
>From my experience the rotary is a very light, compact unit. Its also
very simple from a mechanical standpoint. 
There is a *huge* aftermarket for rotary hi-po add-ons. One of the 
biggest names is Racing Beat 714-779-8677. It is possible to squeeze
200 hp out of the 13B without too much trouble. 
As far as advice goes, if I were gonna bolt a 13B into a 914 I'd
look for a good low mileage turbo motor from a 87 RX-7 turbo and try
to mount that in the car using the Kennedy Engineering kit.


- --
- ----------------------------------------------------------
John Colasante                   johnc@gdstech.grumman.com

Date: Sat, 20 Mar 93 20:48:29 -0600
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: 914 power

>How exacty light is it?  I'm estimating around 2000 lbs.  If so, then I don't 
>think I'll be that suprised.  I've driven in a 302 powered Pinto, a 283 powered
>Austen Healey, a 250 hp Sunbird, a 150 hp VW Bug, a 500 hp 930, a Kellison J-6,
>etc.  To be really suprised, this car would have to weigh under 1000 lbs.  

Hmmm. Well, if you tube frame the car, and do it right, you can lighten it to
under 1600 lbs by doing enough selective surgery to the original tub. Possibly
down to 1400 if you go to fiberglass skins, etc.

>Actually, I've already started building a full tube frame in it.  I know that 
>I want some power, and have heard that 914's have a habit of flexing under hard
>driving (which I have a habit of doing).  I don't see where you came up with 
>the price of $6k, I've built entire tube-frame off-road race machenes for under
>that price.

Ah. I was assuming you'd have to pay to have it done. Doing it youself, it's
just tubes, depreciation on your gear, and whatever your time is worth, natch.

>I'm not familiar with "A" or "S" calipers, however the person I purchased this
>from had already installed an entire 911 system, with stainless lines and 
>braided hoses.  I was assuming that this would be adequate for the lighter 914.
>Do you think that it will be enough?

"S" and "A" calipers are what you get on late 911s, the "S" being alloy and the
"A" being cast iron. How much brakes you need depends on how much mass you're
trying to stop from how fast. In general, for the serious high-horsepower
914 applications I've worked on (admittedly, all of which have been fully-
prepared racers), we've needed the biggest brakes we could get. AND needed
vented rotors at all corners. I dunno -- to me, brakes are kinda like helmets,
where you get the quality/capability commensurate with how you value your
life...

>Well, yes, I know that.  Actually, I was looking (and still am) for someone
>with experience with this type of modification to give me specific spring and
>valving rates.

Sorry, can't help there -- it's all going to depend on what you do to the car
in terms of new weight from the drivetrain, eliminated weight from any cutting
and fitting, etc. No stock answers here. I will say that I'd be really inclined
to get a set of the RSR coil-over struts and adjustable Koni inserts from
Automotion (about $800 a pair) or Carrera coil-overs and Bilstein inserts from
Karftwerke,  and the adjustable height Konis for the rear from Automotion,
because it will make things much easier to re-spring using 2.5" coils than
using torsion bars.

>I was under the impression that 914's were stable and fairly good handling
>cars.  My first choice is the rotary for its weight keeping near the 914
>motor's weight.  Even the weight of using a V-8 is given at 2300 lbs by
>companies who have been doing this for years; I don't think that it would be 
>that unstable with appropriate spring, shock, and sway bars.  I can't see how
>maintance can be that much more expensive than a stock 914 with the exception
>of trans and driveshaft/CV joint breakage because of the added power.  The 
>rotary has low torque (I've seen several in the state of tune I want:
>~200 hp / ~150 lb/ft torque) and so even this should not be a problem.

The 914, and even 914/6 are very stable. What's not "stable" in the 
couldda-had-a-V8 plan is that, well, the tub isn't designed for those
loads. It's not unstable in the sense of wanders around the road, it's
unstable in the sense of positive-feedback-loop, where sooner or later,
you're gonna pop something because of unanticipated load and redistributino
of nodal harmonics due to the new configuration. We even see that in
the EP 914s -- you can only do so much with the tub before it gives up.

>The reason I'm doing this project is not to have a mass-produced sports car.
>I've owned enough of them, and have built enough vehicles from scrach to know
>that this is what I want.  I'd rather not own a car that's just like 
>thousands of others, and I want more performance than that given by these cars
>(when they're stock).  I'm looking for a rather unusual (looking and 
>mechanically), good handling car with about 10 lbs per hp, low emission 
>standards, and a major project is preferred.  In fact, I'm starting easy by
>not building the entire car from scratch.  (which was my original idea).  If 
>anyone else has any ideas, I'd love to hear them.

Well, have you  considered a Beck Sypder? I've got a friend with one, and
it's a blast. Very light, and since you can put anything in it from a
40hp VW Type 1 up through a 3.6 liter Andial-prepped 911 engine, you
should be able to pretty much get your target of 10hp/lb....

They're also VERY unusual, and tend to be big attention getters.

Stan

Date: Sat, 20 Mar 93 20:35:03 -0600
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: 914/6' and various motors

>Are 914/6 2.2's taken straight from early 911 motors?

Well, as someone else pointed out, they are 2.0s, in fact pretty much
exactly 2.0T's, except for three pieces which are only found on the 914/6.
(I know what they are to point at, but can't recall the names tonight. If
it's really important to someone, get back with me next week when my
brain is fully on-line)

>Is there a size difference between the block of a 2.2 and a 2.7, because I know
> that 2.7's used in a few 916's.

The case didn't significantly change size until the 3.2 Carreras. The 2.0, 2.2
and 2.4 case really *IS* the same size, and nearly the same case -- I think
even machined from the same casting but I really don't remember on that part.
The 2.7 case is very different, but still fairly similar.

>Is a 2.7 911 motor just as easily put in a 914 as a 2.2?

Yup. The GT-2 guys put 2.8's in all the time.

>Does anyone have any info on the 914/8's made for Ferry and Ferdinand by the fa
>ctory circa 1973?

There is mention made of it in "Excellence was Expected", and on a couple of
the other Porsche coffeetable books. And you can see it at the factory museum.
There are a couple of 916s (yeah, the real thing) in the US, but only one
is "on display". Can't remember who owns it, but it's either George Hussey or
someone else down in his neck of the woods.

Date: Mon, 22 Mar 93 13:10:46 -0600
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: 914 6 cyl conversions

>I seem to recall that when the 914/6 first came out that the factory
>admitted that the engine ran 15-20F hotter than in the 911T. I assume
>that an extra oil cooler is a good idea for such a conversion, but
>what about cylinder head temperatures, esp. with the higher output
>motors people are using now? Is it advisable to go to a less restrictive
>engine cover also? How about installing a CHT gauge?

My usual rule of thumb is plan on an extra oil cooler at 185 HP. Or,
if you live in some part of the country where you have to worry about
overheating anyway (like Houston). Or if you're going to race it.

You regulate cylinder head temp mainly by cooling the oil, and to some
extent be regulating heat of combustion,i.e. don't run lean or you'll
regret it. Heat -- the number one killer of young air-cooled engines...

The engine cover is, well, another story. You can't put the rain tray
in with a 911 engine in the bay using the "stock" air cleaner setup.
You *can* put the rain tray in if you use K & N air cleaners, but only
the short version. And that doesn't allow enough free space over the tops
of the air horns for the carbs to work right. Of course, the really hot
setup for air filters is the ITG or similar "foam top" things, which don't
have any real restriction over the tops of the car. This is however a real 
problem if you live in an area where you have to worry about rain.

And you can convert to a "916 style" lid, by replacing the solid part with
more steel mesh. And I seem to recall that a door trim strip from an 
Audi 100LS is exactly right for going between the original and the "new"
mesh sections.

Stan

Date: Mon, 22 Mar 93 18:31:45 -0600
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: 912 oil cooler?

>how easy/difficult is it to add an oil cooler to a '69 912?  i don't
>recall ever seeing kits in any of the more popular suppliers' 
>catalogs...

That's because it's not a bolt-on operation. The 912 has a 356 engine,
which has a by-pass oil filter. There's no exposed reliable pressure
line. 

To add an external oil cooler, you have to drill and tap the front
cover (usually doing some welding at the same time), and also drill
and tap the case in one other place. Then you have to block the exit
passage from the oil pump, and can then install both a full-flow oil
filter and an oil cooler in the loop back to the other tapped hole
in the case. This is *MUCH* easier to do on a 912 than a 356 as the
912 has an electric tach and the 356 (except the very last ones) has
a mechanical tach that gets in the way of where you want to drill and tap.

Note you have to take the engine apart to do this.

Best discussion of how to add a full flow filter I've seen lately is
in Duane Stuart's book "The 356 Performance Handbook". But Harry Pellow
has some discussion of that in (I think) "Secrets of the Inner Circle".
Of course, given the commentary from a factory trained master mechanic
who's seen Harry's videos, maybe that should be "Secrets of the Inert Circle"
8{) (/2 ?)

Stan

Date: Tue, 23 Mar 1993 10:26:42 +1000 (EST)
From: Craig Pugsley <c.pugsley@trl.oz.au>
Subject: RE: Porsches w/mazda engs.

Hi there,
I read that there is some interest in rotary to porsche conversions.

A local magazine recently featured and early (68 ish) 911 that had a 13B
turbo engine from an RX7. It was fitted with a carburettor (To solve
injection problems) and was fitted using a VW-rotary kit (I think..) the
reason for this was that the conversion cost less than 1/2 of the cost
of a rebuilt porsche engine. Had approx 200 hp.

I don't know much about 914's at all, but depending on the room around
the engine, a 13B turbo may not fit. In any case, a warmed over 12A
would get anywhere from 170 - 220 hp fairly easily, and would fit
better.


------------------------------

Date: Mon, 22 Mar 93 19:58:11 PST
From: Datsun Dave Coleman <dcoleman@jarthur.Claremont.EDU>
Subject: RE: Porsches w/mazda engs. 

> I don't know much about 914's at all, but depending on the room around
> the engine, a 13B turbo may not fit. In any case, a warmed over 12A
> would get anywhere from 170 - 220 hp fairly easily, and would fit
> better.

	Judging by the 914 rotary conversion I saw in progress, I would say
that a 13B turbo should be no problem.  The only size difference beteween
the 12A and 13B is each rotor housing is 1 cm longer, all other dimensions
are essentially the same.  There is a big size difference when you add a
turbo (rotaries are small, so a turbo is a significant portion of its
size).  If you were to do a turbo rotary 914 I would suggest some
redesigning of the intake system.  There should be no clearance problems
with the turbo itself (I'm thinking of a second generation RX-7 single turbo
engine, I'm not so sure a sequential twin turbo from the 3rd generation
would fit, but I suspect, again, that the turbos themselves would not be the
problem), the problem would come with the intercooler and intake manifold.
On the single turbo enignes, the intercooler sits on top of the engine and
is fed through a hood scoop.  I suspect this would still fit under the
engine cover on a 914, but it wouldn't get much airflow.  Since 914s suck
their cooling air up from under the car (??? am I way off here, or is that
really how they get their air??  It seems that it would work given the low
pressure area just over the engine...) Putting the intercooler on the left
side (away from the manifolds) should provide plenty of airflow and not
interfere with much.  Of course, this makes for a longer inake sytem that
may introduce some lag.
	some comments on high power rotaries: A warmed over 12A putting out
220hp would be very warmed over.  I have expoerienced a 12A with about
275hp, it lived for about 2000 miles before it ate itslef.  It was a full
peripheral port setup with a 51mm downdraft weber on it.  It had two turbo
mufflers and could still be heard over two miles away, it couldn't be
started with the starter, it had to be push started, and it barely ran below
4000 rpm (but boy did it run above 4000!!!!)  It would have melted the
emissions probe if someone stuck it up its tailpipe, and I guarantee that
with a 914 sized exhaust (ie a lot shourter than in the RX-7) you would have
the tailpipes a nice color of red everytime you went for a spin.  If you are
running a rotary on the street and don't want a turbo, I would suggest not 
doing more than street ports.  Peripheral ports are absolutely undriveable, 
and bridge ports tent to break in about 30 - 40k miles.  (I'd be happy to 
explain what all these port setups are if anyone is interested, but since 
this is the Porsche list, I'll just glaze over it until someone asks me 
more).  Street ports with a 48mm sidedraft is good for about 200hp, which
makes for quite an exciting ride in a 2000 lb car (we had one in a Datsun
510, and it moved quite well!)  There is no way to pass emissions with any
of the engines I have mentioned so far, so if you live in a area where this
matters....  A stock RX-7 Turbo II engine is good for 200hp.  With an
adjustable wastegate and lower restriction intake, 250-260 is easily
attainable, and you still pass emissions.
	OK, I'm ranting and raving now, someone shut me up...  I'd be happy
to talk more about rotaries if anyone is interested, but for now, back to
Porsches...

	-Dave

------------------------------

Date: Mon, 22 Mar 93 21:44:38 -0600
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: 912 oil cooler?

>Stan, VW Beetle hotrodders have been doing something like this to Type
>I (Beetle) engines to add full flow filters and auxiliary coolers.
>These kits consist of a modified oil pump gearset and modified oil
>pump front cover which allows plumbing for external lines. Now my
>question is, do you think such a kit can be adapted for use in the
>Porsche motor? If so then it might be the solution for this 912
>without having to tear down the motor. I have seen these kits adver-
>tised in some of the VW hot rod mags like "VW Trends" and "Hot V-Dubs"
>Now don't ask me what might happen if you try to run -12 AN oil
>lines to the front of the car. I don't have any idea if those tiny
>pumps can actually *move* that much oil.

First thing I thought of myself. Turns out that you can use some of
the parts -- like the extrnal lines, and the thing you screw the oil
filter onto, but the interesting stuff like the oil pump cover and
gears and stuff *ARE NOT THE SAME*.

And for the VW kits you mention, you still have to drill and tap the
case. Good references are the HP Books publications called "How to
Hotrod Your VW Engine" (pre-dating the waterpumpers, natch) and "How
to Rebuild Your Aircooled VW Engine" (which is also a *VERY* good 914
engine rebuild reference -- street grade, of course). You have to
drill and tap the main oil galley for the "return" from the external
filter/cooler, and you have to plug the galley leading off of the pump
to the main galley so that oil will leave out via the pump cover. It's
not just a bolt-on deal.

And, you still have the "need more pressure/volume" problem. On VW's
and 914's you want to use the SCAT or Berg high-volume pump (an extra
8mm of pump gear). On the 356, well, *YOU CAN'T*. Fundamental difference
is that on T-1, -3, and -4 engines, the oil pump is a *unit*,where both
the gears, carrier, and outside wall of the pump slide in and out as a
set. On the 356 (well, OK - the three-piece case OHV versions of the 356
for the picky trivia types out there... 8{) ), the third piece of the
case *IS* the outside part of the pump -- there's no way to use longer
gears without *SERIOUS* machine work!

One of the things I've wondered about is the prospect of replacing the
356 oil cooler with a machined plate to take off oil fittings for
pressure/return to make it easier to fit full flow filters and better
coolers. I haven't gotten around to doing this yet, probably mainly
because I don't race 356s. No motivation other than just plain old
curiosity, and with two race cars partially dis-assembled right now,
and races coming up on April 3-4, it's hard to get motivated to play
with this just to see...

But I don't see any other way to do it right without taking the case apart.

Stan

Date: Mon, 22 Mar 93 22:04:17 -0600
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Sebring update

Well, I know some of you are wondering, so I'll go ahead and let you
know what happened:

The ZZ/ProTechnic Racing team took 4th in GTU and 4th in GTP-Lights.
And had there not been some huge logistical botches, would have taken
at least 3rd in GTU, maybe second. Hard to say.

Ghost Racing hot-shoe Haas Fogle (aside: Haas is the guy who when he was
a lot younger raced a VW Beetle 1300cc in SCCA GT-5. He'd go to junk yards,
buy *core engines*, strip off the heads and piston/cylinders, put on these
race grade pistons, heads, and carbs he had, tighten it up and run it 'til
it blew. He's then "repeat as needed". All the way to a couple of divisional
championships.) drove for the GTU team, which was nominally headed up by
ZZ Top drummer Frank Beard. The rest of the driving team consisted of some
guy who writes for AutoWeek (but who really is an OK driver) and Ernie
someone, a Canadian 914/6 GT-2 driver who turned really impressive times.
The GTP-L car was headed up by Sam Shalala from ProTechnik, a local high-dollar
Porsche only service facility, and co-driven by a team of international
second-tier players -- F3000 and karting champions from a couple of  years
back, that sort of thing.

I asked Haas about what is was like. He said that it was kinda like
driving 150 MPH on the freeway in the worst rain you've ever seen, in
the dark, without lights or lane markers, and getting passed every 30
seconds by something moving *REALLY* fast. He put in nearly 3 hours in
the middle of the night in some of the worst of the rain. And had some
really exciting moments earlier in the race, too, for a total of nearly
5 hours of driving.

If you saw the coverage on ESPN, and remember where the Gunnar 966 went
into the wall and moved the concrete barrier a few feet, well, the 966 missed
the nose of the 911 Haas was driving by about two feet. Haas was having
the same problem -- having just changed back to slicks from rains, and 
then the sky opened up again.

The second semi-exciting part was just before the end of the second ESPN
segment -- the one that ended at 1:00 PM EST. The course was under full 
yellow, and the camera is following the pace car around -- and there
the ZZ/ProTechnik 911 is, 3 cars back from the pace car, for like 10
laps. (white 911, with large bluish/redish triangle on the hood) Turns
out that Fangio was just behind Haas in the Toyota, and right on his ass
the whole way, flashing his brights and everything. Now, since it's a yellow,
he can't pass -- but he could harrass Haas into pulling into the pits so
as to get him out of the way, which is *EXACTLY* what he did.

BTW, the GTU 911 had to run nearly as fast under yellow as it did flat-out
just to keep up with the pace. Something to think about...

Some other trivia: they had started the week with a new motor from CMW,
which chunked after less than 30 minutes of use. Was *NOT* built well,
even though they paid serious money for it. Just an FYI for anyone with
an extra $20k laying about thinking about one of the CMW motors... 8{)

There's more details, but as you can imagine, it's hard to filter out what's
interesting and what's not. So that's it for Sebring coverage... Not
sure what the next ZZ/ProTechnik race will be, but Ghost Racing now turns
it's attention to scenic Abilene, TX, for yet another airport course in 
two weeks.  Sigh...

Stan

I don't think you can replace the driveshaft bearings without some 
REALLY special tools.  Plus, they're positioned quite perfectly at the 
shaft resonance nodes, which you might find hard to replicate.  I 
wouldn't touch the tube - just buy a used / new one if your bearings are bad.

I've never heard of this problem in any transaxle Porsche - it seems to 
be the one bearing in the car that is both expensive AND reliable!

		JBS

Date:    Tue, 23 Mar 1993 9:21:32 -0800 (PST)
From: LAFORCE@XENON.ARC.NASA.GOV
Subject: auto-x time-trial rules


A few weeks ago, someone posted a query about auto-x rules and safety
considerations.  I mentioned that the "gang of seven," headed by the
infamous Hank Watts ("Secrets of Solo Racing," Loki Publishing) had
proposed a new set of auto-x / time-trial rules and car classes.  I
also offered to try to get an electronic copy if anyone was interested.

Well, I would have a copy RIGHT NOW, except for the weird brownout
in Sunnyvale last night...  Honest, the disk is in the mail.

I've been autocrossing for 2 years in the Golden Gate and Redwood
Regions of Zone 7.  The new rules are a comprehensive look at the 
current cars and how they stack up against each other.  They are
a **big** improvement over the existing rules.

I think that any region setting up a competitive driving program 
would bennefit from looking these over (perhaps adopting them w/o
change).  For another region, there may be a few things that seem
arbitrary.  There was a successful (IMHO) attempt not to move anyone
into a really weird class or put someone who had worked their car
to the edge of the current rules into the next class.

Since the rules should only be about 50 or 60 disk blocks on a VAX,
I am planning to post them to the list unless I get a lot of requests
not too (or one request from Stan...).


- --Soren            laforce@xenon.arc.nasa.gov

Date: Tue, 23 Mar 93 14:27:09 -0800
From: albrecht@cod.nosc.mil (Robert Albrecht)
Subject: Re: How do easy-outs work?

     Easy-outs aren't magic.  If the bolt broke due excessive
stress or fatigue, you have a chance.  If the bolt broke because
the threads are siezed, then you may break the easy-out in the
broken bolt, making it virtually impossible to drill out the
whole thing and re-tap it (the easy-out being VERY hard/brittle
material).  You may be better off drilling it first, depending -
 - :(

     Any tool shop would have them, even Sears.

Bob
^Z

Date: Tue, 23 Mar 1993 17:30:46 EST
From: pgt@keps.com (Peter Tracy)
Subject: Re: How do easy-outs work?

>
>
>	Howdy all,
>
>	It has been a bad week.  In the last few days I have set a new
>personal record for breaking bolts.  I have heard that Easy-Outs make
>it possible to get broken bolts out fairly easily.  How do they work?
>Also, where can I get them?  I looked in the Snap-On catalog and
>didn't see any.  My application is the rear hinge bolts on the 914
>(and a couple of broken bleader valves on the brakes.)
>

Easy outs look similar to a tap for threading a hole except that
they have a spiral shaped pattern on them instead of threads.
You start by drilling into the broken bolt. Then attach the easy out
to a handle for a tap, insert it into the hold and turn out the
bolt. Of course, if the broken bolt is really frozen/rusted in,
this might not work without heat.

I'd think you could find them anywhere tools are sold, including
Snap-On, but I haven't bought any in years.

Good Luck!!

------------------------------

Date: Tue, 23 Mar 93 14:17:03 PST
From: Datsun Dave Coleman <dcoleman@jarthur.Claremont.EDU>
Subject: Re: Porsches w/mazda engs. (port talk)

> Dave
> Very nice letter.  I was reading about the ports and thinking, would it be too
> rude to ask about that.  And then your () came up asking us to ask, so I am
> asking you to please tell.  The beauty of the Porsche net is technical info.
> Also, what port would work best in my Isuzu pickuptruck?  (just kidding).
> But now I wish i didn't let my father give away that rusty old 912 that I
> had in his garage, keeping his Honda out in the rain). 
> thanks
> Dan

	Well, ok, if anyone opjects to Mazda talk, let me know, but for
now....

	OK, if you don't already have a basic idea how a rotary works, I
might not be able to shed much light on it.  They are hard to describe in
words.  How about a convex triangular rotor precessing in an epitrochoidal
housing.  Yea, that would do it.  Ok, now that we have that out of the way,
the intake and exhaust ports have no valves, they are opened and closed by
the rotor sliding over them.  Because of this, port location and size
becomes the rotary equivalent to cam profile and valve size.  The key to
getting horsepower from a rotary is getting as much air as possible in and
out of the engine, there is almost never any messing around with compression
ratios (except going between 9.5:1 and 8.5:1 (I think) for normally
aspirated or turbocharged engines respecively).  There are no aftermarket
rotors with different combustion pockets (same thing as piston dish)
for different compression, so don't even think about trying to mess with
it.  Nearly all horsepower gains come from messing with the intake ports (a
little can be done to open up the exhaust port, but they flow pretty well
already, exhaust work is usually done downstream in the exhaust system, and
is also *very* beneficial).  The basic ideas of port work are as follows:
	Most rotaries have two intake ports per rotor, one in each side
housing.  They are opened and closed by the side of the rotor sliding over
them.  The closer the ports are to the periphery (the rotor housing), the
longer they will stay open.  Also, if they extend farther towards the top of
the engine, they will close later (good for high rpm power when the intake
air has some inertia).  Of course, port size has the obvious effect, except
that the directions in which the size is increased effect the timing.  Ok,
so those are the basic principles of intake ports, now what's available (you
probably already guessed you don't just want to go after the ports with a
dremel tool and a prayer).
	The 6 port 13B:
		This engine, which is the one found in all normally
aspirated 13B powered RX-7s (second generation cars and first generation
GSL-SE) had one intake port on one side of the rotor and two on the other.
The "auxiliary power port" as the Mazda guys call it was a small port
located above the normal port, so it essentially added some (lots of)
duration.  The port was shut off by means of a butterfly valve except at
high rpm and high loads (the valve was opened based on exhaust pressure).
Under these conditions, the extra duration could be used without hurting
drivability.  This is essentially variable valve timing, but far simpler and
cheaper!
	<so if all 13B powered RX-7s had 6port engines, how do I claim that
most rotaries have four port engines?  Well, ok, most of the standard
modifications are done to the four port engines, because they have been
around longer.  The four port 13B was found in the RX-4 and the Cosmo
(RX-5), and probably some random Japanese models too.>
	Street ports:
		Street ports are essentially someone who knows what they are
doing going after the stock four port engine with a dremel tool (I suggest
Racing Beat, they really know what they are doing).  The intake ports are
enlarged, mostly up and out (towards the perephery) and are smoothed out a
lot.  There is usually a little exhaust port work to go along with it.  With
street ports and an appropriate intake system (48mm DeLorto or Holley 4
barrel) and a header and a free exhaust system, this will give you an engine
that puts out about 200hp, still idles under 1000rpm, and will pull all the
way from idle to wherever you decide to shift (if you shift at or below
8000rpm, the apex seals will last far longer)
	Bridge ports:
		Bridge ports start with street ports, then another long
skinny port about the same height as the street port is cut out of the corner 
of chamber where the side chamber and rotor housing meet.  (a port on the 
periphery is always open!  Only the apex seal slides over these ports, so are 
always open to at least one chamber).  Since the corner seals (and all the
seals for that matter) are spring loaded to keep pressure agaist the rotor
and side housings, blending these two ports into one lartge port would
result in the disasterous sproinging out of the corner seal (yea, the
official Mazda term is sproing..., that's the ticket!).  To avoid this, there
is a "bridge" left between the two ports, hence the name.  The problem with
bridge ports, besides their poor steetability (depending, of course, on your
tolerance for rough low rpm running), is that the bridges tend to crack
after a while.  Because of this, bridge ports are usually reserved for pure
race engines.
	Peripheral Ports:
		Peripheral ports are what they sound like.  They are cut
into the rotor housing instead of the side housings.  The side ports are
welded up, and either a factory Mazda Comp rotor housing, or a Racing Beat
housing (or whoever may make such a housing) must be used.  Since peripheral
ports are open all the time, and are usually HUGE, they aren't happy at all
at low rpm.  They often aren't even happy at high rpm, but if they are good,
they are very very good!  The 275hp 12A I rode in was a peripheral port
setup using the Racing Beat housings.  Peripheral ports have been outlawed
in most racing classes, and nobody seems to complain much.  You can get
amazing amounts of power at high rpm with peripheral ports (think about the
inertia of the intake charge if the ports NEVER close!), but like I
mentioned before, they are very obnoxious engines.

	Some of my info is a few years out of date.  I'm sure there has been
some development in the modifying of 6 port engines since the most recent
stuff I have seen.  Last I heard Racing Beat was very excited about the
prospects of turbocharging a 6 port engine, but hadn't done it yet.  There
has been very little success by anyone to make a good aftermarket turbo for
a rotary, so if you are going to go that route, you should probably stick 
with a mostly stock engine.
	Good intake and exhaust systems are also a must for good power, and
if you plan to spin high rpm (if you are building a rotary, you plan to
spin!), get a breakerless distributor.  The street ported rotary I spend a
summer wrenching on was constantly having ignition problems because of the
points (sometimes with spectacular results, we almost caught the tailight on
fire once when the engine just started belching flames out the exhaust pipe
for about a second!)

 	I hope some of this was clear, I can't tell.  If there is anything I
can clear up (or anything I am wrong about) let me know...

	-Dave

Date: Tue, 23 Mar 93 18:09:38 EST
From: lupienj@wal.hp.com (John Lupien)
Subject: Re: How do easy-outs work?

> 	It has been a bad week.  In the last few days I have set a new
> personal record for breaking bolts.  I have heard that Easy-Outs make
> it possible to get broken bolts out fairly easily.  How do they work?

An easy-out is a left-hand threaded tapered screw. You drill into the bolt,
screw the easy-out into the hole, and then you apply sufficient torque
to it that it snaps off flush, leaving you a stuck bolt with some very hard
steel embedded. They only ever work if the bolt really isn't stuck, but if
that's the case, it shouldn't have snapped off.


- -- 
- ---
John R. Lupien
lupienj@wal.hp.com

------------------------------

Date: Tue, 23 Mar 93 18:25:38 EST
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: How do easy-outs work?


	   It has been a bad week.  In the last few days I have set a new
   personal record for breaking bolts.  I have heard that Easy-Outs make
   it possible to get broken bolts out fairly easily.  How do they work?
   Also, where can I get them?  I looked in the Snap-On catalog and
   didn't see any.  My application is the rear hinge bolts on the 914
   (and a couple of broken bleader valves on the brakes.)

Easy-Outs (or stud extractors) come in at least two forms. I have used
the type that looks like a corkscrew and the type that has a rect-
tangular profile, preferring the later. Mine were made by PROTO, but
many companies make them (Snap-On doesn't? eh?). 

In order to get them to work you have to drill out the center of the
offending stud first. Let's say you're trying to remove an 8mm stud.
You would look on the package that came with the Easy-Out and it would
tell you to use a 3/16" drill for that size stud. So you would drill
out the stud right through the center with the 3/16" bit and then
insert the Easy-Out until it wedged into the stud. Then you'd apply
reverse torque (anti-clockwise) to the Easy-Out and the stud would
turn out easily. That's the ideal situation.

In reality, the Easy-Out is not a godsend. They do work well most of
the time but it takes *careful* drilling with progressively larger
bits and alot of patience. But if that sucker breaks off in the stud
then you'll really go crazy because you can't drill through it, its
probably harder than the drill bit. You have to try to chisel it out
and it gets very ugly very quickly. You'll want to trade the car for
a bicycle and pedal to the funny farm...

- --
- ----------------------------------------------------------
John Colasante                   johnc@gdstech.grumman.com

------------------------------

Date: Tue, 23 Mar 93 15:45:53 PST
From: Jim Harriger <jimharr@microsoft.com>
Subject: RE: How do easy-outs work?

an easy out  (generically called a bolt extractor) is a small piece of 
tool steel,
somewhat tapered, with a set of very coarse, left-handed threads cut into it.
to use it, a hole is drilled into the offending (offensive? :-} ) bolt, 
and the extractor is tapped into the hole, while being threaded into 
the hole. this will usually turn the stuck bolt out of the hole. 
depending on the situation, the hard part can either be drilling the 
hole, or getting the stub unstuck. good luck!

						jim harriger

Subject: re:easy outs


As a few have mentioned, it the bolt is **really** in there good, 
the easy out will break too.

Yes, you cannot drill them.

So, if you think this is the case, soak the broken bolt in liquid 
wrench  for a week or so first, then use the easy out.

Date: Wed, 24 Mar 93 05:34 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: How do easy-outs work?

>In reality, the Easy-Out is not a godsend. They do work well most of
>the time but it takes *careful* drilling with progressively larger
>bits and alot of patience. But if that sucker breaks off in the stud
>then you'll really go crazy because you can't drill through it, its
>probably harder than the drill bit. You have to try to chisel it out
>and it gets very ugly very quickly. You'll want to trade the car for
>a bicycle and pedal to the funny farm...

This is absolutely true.  I broke one, a Sears I think, trying to get
a frozen carburetor jet out a few years ago.  Not happy, no, not at all. 
Ended up getting the smallest drill bit I owned, and arduously drilled
teeny holes all around the jet's crown to get everything out.

Never used the damn thing again, until last summer.  The jam nut (for
wont of the correct term) which holds the easy-out bit in the t-handle
turned out to be just the right size to replace a vanished 7 mm jam
nut on a friend's 911 clutch cable, at the track.  I think the new owner
of the car may still be driving around with a piece of my easy-out.

Kevin

Date: Wed, 24 Mar 93 05:34 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: Message-Id:

>I don't think you can replace the driveshaft bearings without some

>shaft resonance nodes, which you might find hard to replicate.  I

>bad.

>I've never heard of this problem in any transaxle Porsche - it seems to 
>be the one bearing in the car that is both expensive AND reliable!

In fact, the drive tube bearings are not replaceable.  If one is gone,
you are looking at a pretty expensive replacement of the entire tube.
Fortunately, I too have never heard of one failing.

Date: Wed, 24 Mar 93 08:46:42 -0800
From: albrecht@cod.nosc.mil (Robert Albrecht)
Subject: Re: 911 padded dash removal

     The padded dash unit sits on top of some conformal sheet
metal.  There are several fasteners.  The padded dash has a few
studs which fit through holes in the sheetmetal, secured by nuts,
and also a handful of plastic clips which poke through holes in
the sheetmetal and snap into place.  The latter are a pain to
remove.  Access to the nuts and the clips is from the trunk for
some, and by removing a dash instrument or two for others.  Be
careful in your selection of a shop, as clearance is limited, and
groping around behind the dash without care can damage wireing,
etc.  Point out that everything works, and you expect the same
when they are finished!

     The book says to remove the windshield for dash replacement.
I did it with the windshield in place.  This was a struggle.
Someone experienced in glass work would find removing the
windshield to be a time saver.

Bob
^Z

Date: Wed, 24 Mar 93 12:07:38 -0500
From: js@aixservr.egr.duke.edu (Jeff Shorey)
Subject: Re: 911 padded dash removal

Just out of curiousity, how much were you quoted for the restoration of your
dash and by whom?  My dash is really shot and I've been thinking about doing
the same thing in leiu of buying a $850 new replacement.

To remove the dash, you'll probably have to remove the instruments, radio, and
glove box to get to some of the bolts (I've done it on a 914).  Also, you may
have to come in from the front trunk to get to some of the forward ones.  I've
always heard that you have to remove the windshield to get the dash out - don't
know if it's true or not.

My rear defroster doesn't work either - the lines on the window have corroded
away to nothing.  Take an ohmmeter and check the resistance across the lines -
it should be on the order of a few ohms.  Mine reads around 20K...

 - Jeff Shorey  js@egr.duke.edu

Date: Wed, 24 Mar 93 10:23:52 -0800
From: John Wright <jpw@hpsadlb.sr.hp.com>
Subject: 911 padded dash removal

>From: Mark Blaskovich <blaskovi@cisco.com>
>Date: Wed, 24 Mar 93 8:02:51 PST
>
>I kind of want to let the shop remove/recover/install the dash as then I
>can hold them accountable for a good looking nice fitting finished 
>product, but would either like to give them some clues or even help them
>get the dash out.  
>
>What are the clues or tricks I need to know?
>
>Oh, another thing.  My rear window defroster doesn't work.  The light comes
>on when you pull out the 2 position knob, but no defrost action.  Last
>check of the fuses showed all ok.
> 
>thanks,
>Mark

The padded dash has several molded in bolts. There are 2? above 
the instrument cluster and 3? from the front edge of the dash, with
one at the extreme left and one at the extreme right.

The edge nearest the windshield is usually held on by sheet metal screws
from above.

Also there are a number of clips similar to the ones that hold the
door panels on.  You will need to pull out your fresh air fan and
maybe the windshield wiper assembly to get to all the fasteners.
If you pull the windshield wiper, be sure and mark where all the
wires go before you remove it.

Usually, the front windshield has to be removed to do the job.

My guess is the rear window defroster doesn't work because the
elements are shot.  You can verify that voltage is getting to the
elements by probing at the feed points under the rear window gasket.
See if you are getting 12-14 volts.  The car must be running and the
alternator working to make the measurement because the defroster voltage
feed is diode isolated from the battery.

If the voltage is there, turn off the defroster switch and 
measure the resistance of the elements with a voltmeter with the defroster
switch off.  

If I remember correctly, the elements are split up into three sections,
a middle, and top and bottom.  If the middle section is rated for
100 watts, expect resistance of about (V^2)/100 = 144/100= 1.44 ohms.

a couple of hundred RPM before it bounces back up to 800 RPM.  This rough

Would the car still run if the Air Flow Meter was dead?  

I saw the messages about the DME test connector and how to get 
information out of it with a LED.   I was hoping the DME knows 
what is wrong.   I looked for it on my car and could not find it.

a much larger connector.  Does anybody know if my car has the test 
connector that outputs information to a LED and where EXACTLY it might be?

recommend to get a used one?  How much $$ am I looking at?  
Any input you have is appreciated!

------------------------------

Date: Thu, 25 Mar 93 02:02 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: 944-S Rough Idle

David -

It is very unlikely that your air flow meter has suddenly and unexpectedly
died.  More likely, you have crud in your idle stabilizer.  Alas, getting
to it on the 944S is the pits, worse than any other model 944 or 968
because of the size of the "bunch of bananas."  I recommend removing it
and cleaning it out with carb cleaner, as a first step.  If that doesn't
solve the problem, you can start to look for other causes.

Kevin

Date: Thu, 25 Mar 93 09:36 CST
From: rondl@ihlpl.att.com
Subject: dash removal


Re:  Padded dash removal
For my '66 912, you have to remove the windshield, and I suspect with
later models this is still true.  You dont have to remove any instruments
to get it out.  There's just a few nuts/clips and its a easy operation.
I dont think there's any way to get the dash out without taking your
windshield out (back of dash flips up and would not pass the windshield).

In Hemming's there a numerous adds to have your dash recovered,
several have experience with P-cars.  I got estimates around $250 and
plan on having one of them do the work within the next few weeks.
You just ship it to 'em, they call you with an estimate and then do the work
and ship it back.  Most say it takes a couple of weeks.


Ron De Lange rondl@ihlpl.att.com
Proud owner of a '66 912 with new:  inner/outer rockers, floor pan (almost),
door jams, hinge posts, trailing arm repair kits, and a kajillion patches!

Date: Fri, 26 Mar 93 11:08:20 PST
From: Jim Selders <jimseld@microsoft.com>
Subject: RE: 944 hatch adjustment

Loosen the lock nut, and spin the lock pin up/down as needed.  If it's 
too tight, the hatch may nut latch securely and might actually squeak 
more.  Too loose won't seal well, but it'll generally be safe.

NO, don't loosen the star allen's.

Flush dirt from the latches by spraying WD-40 (or similar oil-based 
juice) into the latch.  Then, lubricate the pins and latches with 
grease.  Don't put too much on the pins - you'll get it on your 
clothes, etc. when you use the hatch.

		JBS

Date: Sat, 27 Mar 93 02:03 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: 944 hatch adjustment

I would do everything as Jim Selders suggests.  You can also check the
star allen bolts for tightness.  I think they take a 6 mm bit, which is
smaller than the 8 mm most folks have for doing CV joints.

Lube the hinges at the top of the hatch; something like Wurth HHS 2000
is great because it penetrates then dries.  Check all the Philips head
screws retaining the air dam.  Finally, check the rear wiper motor
assembly.  The two grommets which hold it to the window frame tend to
decay.  Make sure the rubber seals around the armature are in position.

(I found the factory had thrown an extra fender washer on the inside of
the armature where it goes through the window.  I removed it.)

One source of squeaking is apparently the fit of the window in the frame.
A friend tried fixing his by shimming thin cardboard between the trim
and the window glass.  Temporary fix, since it ended up just pushing
the frame further from the glass.

When in doubt, lube.

Kevin

------------------------------

Date: Sat, 27 Mar 1993 00:41:19 -0500 (EST)
From: Tony Matt <tonym@world.std.com>
Subject: No Longer Shiftless


Well, I went and did the clutch master and slave on the 924S last
Saturday.  I wasn't going to, but the shop estimates exceeded my
threshold of pain, and GPR had the parts for $96 (yes, they're
the same as on the 931).  Even with UPS Red, they had the best
price by far (Strano's was next, others about 50% higher).
Matched the originals exactly (except for the date code ;-).

Changing them was somewhat of a hassle, mostly because I worked
on them in my driveway in 40-45 degree weather.  I made a lot
of dumb mistakes, like nicking the spoiler when trying to get
the front wheels on ramps, and connecting the hoses to the master
before the slave was in place, such that the reservior emptied 
out onto the driveway.  The most difficult part of the job was
loosening the starter bolts; I finally got the right combination
of ratchet and extender to crack them.  BTW, is there a torque
specification for these bolts, or the fasteners for the master
and slave cylinders?  I didn't see any in the manual.  The next
hardest part was removing/replacing the nuts that fasten the 
master - it's pretty tight in there, so progress was slow.

The entire job took me about six hours, not including the bleed
that I still need to do.  It turned out to be relatively 
straightforward, although next time (should there be one) will
be much easier, now that I've gone through it.  Total parts
cost was about $140.  I'll use the money I saved to buy a 
bleeder, and a...

Regards,
Tony Matt   tonym@world.std.com

Date: Sun, 28 Mar 93 03:45 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: No Longer Shiftless

Tony -

clutch slave cylinder to clutch housing: M8 x 28, 21 Nm, 15 ftlb
clutch master cylinder to bulkhead: M8, 21 Nm, 15 ftlb
clutch tube to clutch hose or to master and slave cylinder: M12, 16 Nm,
 12 ftlb

I don't know the size of the starter bolts, and there is no specific
torque value listed for them.  Which means use the defaults:

M6: 8 + 2 Nm, 6 + 1.5 ftlb
M8: 20 + 2 Nm, 15 + 1.5 ftlb
M10: 40 + 5 Nm, 29 + 4 ftlb

Congrats on the work done!  I recommend using/borrowing a power bleeder
to finish the job, if possible.  Actually, I seem to remember that you
live on or real close to a steep hill.  That being the case, you can
use the hill to get the slave cylinder tilted so you can get the air
up near the bleeder nipple.

Kevin

Date: Sun, 28 Mar 93 19:18 GMT

Subject: Chassis dyno in the Northeast

Given periodic questions about available dynamometers, as well as general
personal excitement, I thought I'd pass this along.  My friends at
European Performance Engineering in Natick, Mass. now have their brand
spanking new Clayton chassis dyno up, running, and available.  They
would be happy to help fellow enthusiasts sort the horsepower from the
horsepoop on their cars.

This may be a really good time to take advantage of the dyno, because
they aren't quite sure what they should be charging folks for its use.

We put my car on it yesterday.  Very strange to be flooring the gas
pedal, incredible racket, but sitting still!  The exhaust manifold, despite
a huge, Cecile B. DeMile-style fan, was quite cherry red.

They popped for the 500 hp. model.  This is 500 hp. max at the rear
wheels.  It was surprising to learn just how much horsepower is typically
lost from the engine to the driving wheels.

Anyhow, sorry for the wasted bandwidth to those of you outside the area.
Anyone interested, give Jerry a call at 508 651 1316 (and mention my name).

Kevin

Date: 29 Mar 93 16:27:00 EDT
From: "DEHAAS, MARK" <dehaasm@NAPC-1.NAVY.MIL>
Subject: Easy outs one more time



I don't want to beat a dead horse but I found out something that might
help the guy who asked about easy outs.  I recently found out that there
is a left hand drill bit.  Instead of using the standard drill bit to 
drill a hole in the broken stud or bolt use a drill bit that spins
counterclockwise.  You'll need a reversible drill obviously and by drilling
in the opposite direction the vibrations sometimes will loosen the broken
stud and make the easy out unnecessary.  If you still need to use the
easy out at least drilling counterclockwise will not have made the
situation worse.  I have no idea where to get these drill bits but
a large mill and machine would probably have them.

Mark deHaas
dehaasm@napc-1.navy.mil

Date: Mon, 29 Mar 93 16:10:12 PST
From: Rod.Miller@Eng.Sun.COM (Rod Miller)
Subject: RE: lease termination

Your options depend on the type of lease you have. First, a simplistic
and hopefully still accurate primer on lease types:

1. A car lease is like buying something on time - you start out with a
principal amount that is owed (in leasing it is called the capitalized
cost, but I won't use that term here.) Part of your monthly payments go
towards interest and part towards principal. However, at the end of a
lease, there is a non-zero principal amount remaining.

2. With a _closed end_ lease, the leasing company/dealer takes the risk
that at lease termination, the lease principal amount will be greater
than the car's market value.  Closed end leases are good for the lessor
(you) if the car is worth less than the principal amount - you walk
away and let the leasing company/dealer take the bath.

3. With an _open end_ lease, you, the lessor, take the risk that that
at lease terminatinon, the lease principal amount will be greater than
the car's market value.  These are good if the car is worth more than
the principal amount - you can buy the car for the principal amount and
resell it at a profit if you want. If the car's value is less than the
market value, you take the bath.

If you have a closed end lease and you want the car, negotiate with the
leasing company/dealer. They are stuck, and a sale to you at a loss
would be much simpler than taking the car back and either reselling it
or sitting on it until it sells, both at a guaranteed loss. I read
recently (on this list? - red face if so!) of a situation where the
stuckee dealer approached the lessor about a reduced buyout price (but
too late as the lessor had already committed to another vehicle.)

Good luck!

Rod
